06 SamskÄras

Gautama (II. 1) says that before {{upanayapa|upanayana}}, a boy may act, speak and eat as and what he likes (i. e. may follow his inclinations). HÄradatta explains that this does not mean that he can kill a {{brÄhmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} or drink liquor, but that there is no restriction, although he be a {{brÄhmana’s|brÄhmaá¹‡a’s}} son, to his selling what is forbidden to a {{brÄbmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} to sell, or he may eat onions and garlic or stale food or may eat four or five times a day. {{Ap. Dh. S.|Ä€p. Dh. S.}} (II. {{1. 6. 15. 17-20|6.15.17-20}}) states several views on this point. ‘Up to the time when they begin to take cooked food infants do not become impure (by the touch of a {{rajasvala &o.|rajasvalÄ, etc.}}); according to some (teachers) up till they are one year old; or till they are not able to distinguish the cardinal points; another view is that till {{upanayang|upanayana}} (they do not become impure)’. AparÄrka (p. 28) also explains that a boy may (before {{upa nayana|upanayana}}) eat the leavings of the food of his parents, but he cannot eat or drink what would cause loss of caste as in that case he may become unfit to have the {{samskÄra|saá¹ƒskÄra}} of upanayana performed on him. The {{SinrtyarthasÄra|Smá¹›tyarthasÄra}} gives the view of some that in case an infant touches a cÄá¹‡á¸Äla before it reaches the age of taking cooked food, only water need be sprinkled on it, before caula Äcamana need be done by it and after caula (and before upanayana) a bath would be necessary. {{Vas. Dh. 8.|Vas. Dh. S.}} (II. 6) quotes a {{verge|verse}} of {{Harita|HÄrÄ«ta}} to the effect ‘up till {{investi ture|investiture}} with the girdle of {{muÃµja|muÃ±ja}} grass (i. e. till upanayana) there is no action that is obligatory on him, as long as he is not born again for Vedic study he may be in his {{conduot|conduct}} like a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}}’.1 This verse occurs also in {{Baud. Dh. S.|Baudh. Dh. S.}} I. 2. 6 and Manu II. 171 and 172. {{Daksa|Daká¹£a}} I. 3-4 {{gays|says}} ‘Till a boy is eight years old he is like one newly born and only indicates the caste in which he is born. As long as his upanayana is not performed the [[189]] boy {{inours|incurs}} no blame as to what is allowed or forbidden to be eaten, as to what should (or should not) be drunk, as to what he should or should not speak, as to telling a falsehood’.2 But this does not hold good as to {{mahÄpatakas|mahÄpÄtakas}}. As to {{prÄya soitta|prÄyaÅ›citta}} when a child is guilty of the commission of a {{mah.. pÄtaka|mahÄpÄtaka}}, {{sea|see}} under prÄyaÅ›citta later on and the Mit. on {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} III. 253.

The {{siná¹­tis|smá¹›tis}} look upon {{upanayang|upanayana}} as the second birth of a boy (the first being his physical birth).3 Gaut. (X. 1 and 51) says that the three higher classes are called dvijÄtis (having two births), while the {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} is only {{ekajÄli|ekajÄti}}.4 {{Ap. Dh. S,|Ä€p. Dh. S.}} says (I. 1. 1. 16-18), ’the teacher causes him (the boy who is initiated into Vedic study) to be born from vidyÄ (i.e. by imparting Vedic knowledge), that birth is superior, the parents produce only the body’.5 Upanayana is like Baptism and St. John (3. 3) also says ’except a man be born again, he cannot see the {{Kingdom|kingdom}} of God.’ Manu II. 147-148 (= {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh, S.|Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} 30. 45-46) convey the same idea. Manu (II. 169) speaks of three births in the case of a man, first birth from his mother, the second when the girdle is tied (i.e. on {{upana yana|upanayana}}) and the third when he is initiated for a Vedic sacrifice. Upanayana is the foremost of the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}. Atri (141-142) says ‘a person is known as a {{brÄbmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} by birth, he is said to be a dvija (twice-born) on account of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}, he reaches the position of a vipra by learning (study of the Veda); he is called Å›rotriya on account of all these three’. ParÄÅ›ara (VIII. 19) contains a fine image to illustrate this ‘just as a work of painting gradually unfolds itself on account of the several colours (with which it is drawn), so {{brahmanya|brÄhmaá¹‡ya}} (the status of a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}}) is similarly brought out by {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} performed according to prescribed rites.’6

Therefore it is now time to speak of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}. [[190]]

The word saá¹ƒskÄra hardly ever occurs in the ancient Vedic literature, but the root â€œ{{kf|ká¹›}}’ with ‘sam’ and the past passive participle ‘{{samsksta|saá¹ƒská¹›ta}}’ occur often enough. In á¹šg. V. 76. 2 the word {{samsksta|saá¹ƒská¹›ta}} is applied to ‘gharma’ (vessel) ’the two AÅ›vins do not harm the gharma that has been purified.’ In á¹šg. VI. 28. 4 we have the word ‘{{sanskrtatra|saá¹ƒská¹›tatra}}’ and á¹šg. VIII. 33. 9 has ‘{{ranÄya samskrtah|raá¹‡Äya saá¹ƒská¹›taá¸¥}}’. {{Sat.|Åšat.}} Br. I. 1. 4. 10 speaks of preparing (or purifying) {{offering|the offering}} (havis) for the gods. So in Åšat. Br. III. 2. 1. 22 ’therefore a woman approaches a man who stands in a well-trimmed ({{samskÃ½ta|saá¹ƒská¹›ta}}) house.’7 Vide {{VÄj. S.|VÄj. S.}} IV. 34 for a similar use of {{samaksta|saá¹ƒská¹›ta}}. In {{Chan. Up.|ChÄn. Up.}} IV. 16. 2, we read “of that sacrifice there are two ways, by mind, by speech; the BrahmÄ (priest) prepares (or polishes) one of them by his mind.”8 The word saá¹ƒskÄra is used several times in the sÅ«tras of Jaimini (as in III. 1. 3, III. 2. 15 and 17, III. 8. 3, IX.2. 9, 42, 44, IX. 3. 25, IX. 4. 33, IX. 4. 50 and 54, X. 1. 2 and 11 &c.). It generally means some purificatory {{aot|act}} in a sacrifice e. g. in Jaimini III. 8. 3 the word is applied to the actions of shaving the head, washing the teeth and paring the nails on the part of the sacrificer in {{Jyotiá¹£toma|Jyotiá¹£á¹­oma}}; in IX. 3, 25 the word saá¹ƒskÄra is applied to proká¹£aá¹‡a (sprinkling with water), in X. 2. 49 it is applied to the {{sharing|shaving}} of the head and face. In Jaimini9 VI. 1. 35 the word saá¹ƒskÄra stands for upanayana. {{Sabara|Åšabara}} explains10 saá¹ƒskÄra as that which being effected makes a certain thing or person fit for a certain purpose and the {{Tantra vÄrtika|TantravÄrtika}} says that {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} are those actions and rites that impart fitness, and it further says,11 “fitness is of two kinds; it arises by the removal of taints (sins) or by the generation of fresh qualities. {{SamskÄras|Saá¹ƒskÄras}} generate fresh qualities, while tapas brings about the removal of taints. He who performs such sacrifices as {{Jyotiá¹£toma|Jyotiá¹£á¹­oma}} and others has certain blemishes in him due to not doing in this life or a previous life duties laid down for him or doing what is forbidden. If they (blemishes) are not removed they obstruct the (acquisition of the) reward of the sacrifice even if it be entirely free from any defects whatever, as they (blemishes) produce (for the sacrificer) the experience of their own fruits that are opposed to the (fruit of the) sacrifice.’

The {{Viramitrodaya|VÄ«ramitrodaya}}12 (on {{sarskÄra|saá¹ƒskÄra}}) defines ‘saá¹skÄra’ as ‘a peculiar excellence due to the performance of rites ordained (by the Å›Ästra) which resides either in the {{Boul|soul}} or the body’ and says that it is of two kinds, one kind making a person eligible for performing other actions (e. g. upanayana renders a person eligible for Vedic study), while another kind removes the evil taint that may have been {{gene rated|generated}} (e. g. {{Jatakarma|JÄtakarma}} removes the taint due to seed and uterus). The word {{saá¹›skÄra|saá¹ƒskÄra}} does not {{ocour|occur}} in most of the {{grhyasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}} (it {{ocours|occurs}} in {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}}), but it {{ocours|occurs}} in the DharmasÅ«tras (vide Gaut. VIII. 8, Ä€p. Dh. S. I. 1. 1. 9, Vas. IV. 1). [[191]]

The principal matters that fall to be discussed under {{ganskaras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} are: the purpose of {{sarskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}, the classification of {{BamskÄras|Saá¹ƒskÄras}}, the number of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}, the procedure of each of the {{saá¹skÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} and the persons authorized to perform them and the persons for whom they are to be performed.

First as to the purpose of {{saá¹‡skÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}. {{Mann|Manu}} (II. 27-28) says ‘In the case of dvijÄtis, the taints (or sins) due to seed and the uterus (i. e. derived from parents) are wiped off by the homas (burnt oblations) performed during pregnancy and by {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}} (ceremonies on birth), caula (tonsure) and the tying of the girdle of muÃ±ja grass. This (human) body is rendered fit for the attainment of brahma by the study of the Veda, by (observance of) vratas, homas (oblations in fire), by the vrata called traividya, by worship (of gods, sages and {{manos|manuá¹£yas}}), by generation of sons, by the performance of the five daily {{saorifices|sacrifices}} and by (solemn Vedic) sacrifices.’ The view of {{Yaj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} (I. 13) is that ’thus (i.e. by the performance of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}) the taint arising from the seed and uterus (i. e. from the physical defects of parents) is removed.’ These words of Manu and {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} are variously interpreted by the commentators. MedhÄtithi says ‘seed and uterus are not the causes of sin and therefore all that is meant by enas (in Manu II. 27) is impurity.’ KullÅ«ka explains that blemishes of seed are those arising from intercourse in a prohibited manner and the ‘{{garbhika|gÄrbhika}}’ blemish is what arises from having to stay in the womb of an impure mother. The Mit.13 on {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 13 makes it clear that {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} are deemed to remove bodily defects transmitted from parents ({{euch|such}} as defective limbs, diseases &c.) and are not intended to remove the taint of being born of sinful parents. Manu II. 66 also states that all the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} are performed on a woman also for the purification of the body. {{HÄrsta|HÄrÄ«ta}}14 as quoted in the {{SamskÄratattva|Saá¹ƒskÄratattva}} says ‘when a person has intercourse according to the procedure of {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} he establishes in the wife a foetus that becomes fit for the reception of the Veda, by the rite of {{punsavana|puá¹ƒsavana}} he makes the garbha become a male, by the ceremony of {{Simantounayana|SÄ«mantonnayana}} he removes from the foetus the taint derived from the parents and the {{accumu lated|accumulated}} taints (which are five) due to seed, blood and womb are removed by {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}}, {{nÄmakarana|nÄmakaraá¹‡a}}, {{annaprÄÅ›ana|annaprÄÅ›ana}}, {{cÅ«dÄkarana|cÅ«á¸Äkaraá¹‡a}} and samÄvartana. By these eight {{sam,kÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} (from {{garbha. dhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}) purity arises.’ The exact significance of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} in the development of higher human personality was left rather vague in our authorities and their treatment of the purpose of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} is not very elaborate or exhaustive. The {{samskaras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} had been treated from very ancient times as necessary for unfolding the latent capacities of man for development and as being the outward symbols or signs of the inner change {{whioh|which}} would fit human beings for corporate life and they also tended to confer a certain status on those who underwent them. If we look at the list of {{sarskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} we shall find that the purposes of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} were manifold. Some like {{Upanayang|Upanayana}} served spiritual and cultural purposes, they brought the unredeemed person into the company of the elect, they opened the door to Vedic study and thus conferred special privileges and exacted duties. They have also psychological values impressing on the mind of the person that he has assumed a new role and must strive to observe its rules. Other {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} like {{namakarapa|nÄmakaraá¹‡a}}, {{annapraÅ›ana|annaprÄÅ›ana}}, and {{niskramana|niá¹£kramaá¹‡a}} were more or less of a popular nature. They afforded opportunities for the expression of love and affection and for festivities. Other {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} like {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}, {{pursevana|puá¹ƒsavana}}, {{simantonnayana|sÄ«mantonnayana}} had also mystical and symbolical elements. VivÄha (marriage) was a sacrament which brought about a union of two personalities into one for the purpose of the continuance of society and for the uplift of the two by self-restraint, by self-sacrifice and mutual co-operation. [[192]]

The {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} were divided by {{HÄrlta|HÄrÄ«ta}} into two kinds, {{brahma|brÄhma}} and daiva.15 The {{saraskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} of {{garbhadhana|garbhadhÄna}} and others which are described only in the smá¹›tis are called {{brÄbma|brÄhma}} and the man who is purified by performing them attains equality with sages, stays in the same world with them and is joined with them; {{pakayajÃ±as|pÄkayajÃ±as}} (offerings of cooked food), yajÃ±as with burnt offerings and sacrifices in which soma is offered are called daiva ({{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}). The last two varieties, viz. those in which there is burnt offering and those in {{wbich|which}} Soma is offered, are dealt with in the {{frauta sÅ«tras|Å›rauta sÅ«tras}}, which have been left outside the purview of this work (except in the note at the end of this volume). [[193]]

There is a great divergence of views among the writers on {{smrtis|smá¹›tis}} as to the number of {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}. Gaut. (VIII. 14-24) speaks of forty {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} and eight virtues of the soul. The forty {{samskÄras ars|saá¹ƒskÄras are}}: {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}, {{pumaavana|puá¹ƒsavana}}, sÄ«mantonnayana; {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}}, {{nÄmakarana|nÄmakaraá¹‡a}}, {{annapraÅ›ana|annaprÄÅ›ana}}, caula, upanayana (8 in all), the four vratas of the Veda, snÄna (or {{gamÄvartana|samÄvartana}}), vivÄha, five daily mahÄyajÃ±as (for deva, {{pits, manusya|pitá¹›s, manuá¹£yas}}, bhÅ«ta and brahma); seven {{pakayajÃ±as|pÄkayajÃ±as}}16 (viz. {{aá¹£taka|aá¹£á¹­akÄ}}, {{pÄrvapasthÄllpaka|pÄrvaá¹‡asthÄlÄ«pÄka}}, Å›rÄddha, {{srÄvapl|Å›rÄvaá¹‡Ä«}}, {{agrahÄyani|ÄgrahÄyaá¹‡Ä«}}, caitrÄ«, {{aÅ›va yuji|ÄÅ›vayujÄ«}}); seven {{haviryajÃ±as|haviryajÃ±as}} (in which there is burnt offering but no soma) viz. {{AgnyÄdbeya|AgnyÄdheya}}, Agnihotra, {{DarÅ›apÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa|DarÅ›apÅ«rá¹‡amÄsa}}, {{Agrayana|Ä€grayaá¹‡a}}, {{Caturmasyas|CÄturmÄsyas}}, {{NirudhapaÅ›ubandha|NirÅ«á¸hapaÅ›ubandha}} and {{SautrÄmapi|SautrÄmaá¹‡i}}); seven soma sacrifices ({{Agniá¹£toma|Agniá¹£á¹­oma}}, {{Atyagniá¹£toma|Atyagniá¹£á¹­oma}}, {{Uktbya|Ukthya}}, {{á¹¢odasin|á¹¢oá¸aÅ›in}}, {{VÄjapeya|VÄjapeya}}, AtirÄtra, {{Ä€ptoryÄma|Ä€ptoryÄma}}). Gautama uses the word saá¹ƒskÄra in the most extended sense. {{Saá¹…kba|Åšaá¹…kha}} as quoted by the Sm. C. (I. p. 13) and the SubodhinÄ« on Mit. II. 4 follow Gautama. {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}} speaks of eighteen {{sÄrira samskÄras|Å›ÄrÄ«ra saá¹ƒskÄras}} (in which he includes {{utthina|utthÄna}}, {{pravÄsÄgamana|pravÄsÄgamana}}, {{piá¹‡davardhana|piá¹‡á¸avardhana}}, which are seen nowhere else as {{saá¹‡skÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}) and twenty-two yajÃ±as (i. e. five daily yajÃ±as as one and seven {{pÄkayajÃ±as|pÄkayajÃ±as}}, seven {{havir-yajÃ±as|haviryajÃ±as}}, and seven Soma yajÃ±as). Most {{grhyasÅ«trag, dharmasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras, dharmasÅ«tras}} and {{smstis|smá¹›tis}} do not enumerate so many. {{Angiras|Aá¹…giras}} (quoted in the {{SamskÄra mayÅ«kba|Saá¹ƒskÄramayÅ«kha}}, {{SamskÄra-prakÄÅ›a|Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a}} p. 135 and other digests) mentions twenty-five {{samskÄrag|saá¹ƒskÄras}}. They {{inolude|include}} all {{sarskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} of Gautama from {{garbhÄdhana|garbhadhÄna}} to the five daily yajÃ±as (which latter are reckoned as one saá¹ƒskÄra by {{Angiras|Aá¹…giras}}) and after {{nÄmakarana|nÄmakaraá¹‡a}} {{niskramana|niá¹£kramaá¹‡a}} is added. Besides, {{Viá¹£á¹‡ubali|Viá¹£á¹‡ubali}}, {{Agrayana|Ä€grayaá¹‡a}}, {{Aá¹£takÄ|Aá¹£á¹­akÄ}}, {{SrÄvapl|ÅšrÄvaá¹‡Ä«}}, {{AÅ›vayuji|Ä€Å›vayujÄ«}}, {{MÄrgasirá¹£i|MÄrgaÅ›Ä«rá¹£Ä«}} (same as {{AgrahÄyaá¹‡i|Ä€grahÄyaá¹‡Ä«}}), {{PÄrvana|PÄrvaá¹‡a}}, Utsarga and {{UpÄkarms|UpÄkarma}} are enumerated as the remaining {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} by {{Angiras|Aá¹…giras}}. {{Veda-VyÄsa . 14-15|Veda-VyÄsa I. 14-15}} enumerates sixteen {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}. Manu, {{Yaj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}}, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S,|Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. This last one is not treated of in Gautama and several {{grh yasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}}. In most of the digests the principal {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} are said to be sixteen; but there is some difference of opinion even as to these sixteen.17 For example, {{JÄtÅ«karnya|JÄtÅ«kará¹‡ya}} as quoted in {{Sam. These slightly differ from the sixteen of {{Veda-Vyisah|VedavyÄsa}}. [[194]]

Number of Samskaras

The {{grhyasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}} deal with {{SamskÄras|Saá¹ƒskÄras}} in two different sequences. Many of them begin with vivÄha (marriage) and then proceed up to samÄvartana. Some like the {{Hiranya kesigrhya|Hiraá¹‡yakeÅ›igá¹›hya}}, {{BhÄradyÄjagá¹£hya|BhÄradvÄjagá¹›hya}} and {{MÄnavagÄ¼hya|MÄnavagá¹›hya}} begin with upanayana.18 Some {{saá¹‡skÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} like {{Karnavedha|Kará¹‡avedha}} and {{Vidya rambha|VidyÄrambha}} are conspicuous by their absence in the {{grbyasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}}, but are added by later {{smstis|smá¹›tis}} and purÄá¹‡as. The following is the list of all the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} usually so called in most of the smá¹›ti works together with a few remarks against each as to the work or works in {{whioh|which}} each is mentioned or described. The {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} are arranged in the sequence of the times at which in a man’s life they are performed beginning from {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}: [[195]]

{{Rtu-sargamana|á¹štu-saá¹ƒgamana}}â€”mentioned in {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}}19 I. 1 as distinct from {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}; it calls it {{niá¹£eka|niá¹£eka}} also (VI. 2) and describes it in III. 9 and {{garbhÄdbÄna|garbhadhÄna}} in III. 10. The {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}} commences the {{saá¹skÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} with {{niá¹£eka|niá¹£eka}}.

{{GarbhadhÄna|GarbhadhÄna}}: {{Nizeka|Niá¹£eka}}: {{Caturthikarma or-homa|CaturthÄ«karma or -homa}}â€” Manu (II. 16 and 26), {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 10-11, {{Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. 8.|Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S.}} (2. 3 and 27.1) employ the word {{niá¹£oka|niá¹£eka}} as equivalent to {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}. In the {{Saá¹…khim yana gÄ¼hya|ÅšÄá¹…khÄyana-gá¹›hya}} (I. 18-19), {{PÄr. gr.|PÄr. Gá¹›.}} I. 11, and {{Ap. 85.|Ä€p. The {{Baud. 87.|Baudh. Gá¹›.}} (IV. 6. 1), the {{KathakagÄ¼hya|KÄá¹­hakagá¹›hya}} (30.8), Gaut. (VIII. 14), {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 11 employ the word {{garbha dhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}. According to {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}} (III. 10) the {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} rite follows {{niá¹£eka|niá¹£eka}} or {{stu-samgamana|á¹›tu-saá¹ƒgamana}} (union of married pair after menstruation) and consists in ensuring conception.

{{Pursavana|Puá¹ƒsavana}}â€”occurs in almost all the {{gÄ¼hyasÄtras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}}, in Gaut., in {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} (I. 11).

{{Garbharaká¹£ana|Garbharaká¹£aá¹‡a}}â€”mentioned in the {{Å›ÄnkhÄyana gr.|ÅšÄá¹…khÄyana-gá¹›hya}} (1. 21). It seems to be the same as the {{Anavalobhang|Anavalobhana}} which according to the {{AÅ›valÄyana gr.|Ä€Å›valÄyana-gá¹›hya}} (I. 13. 1) occurs in the Upaniá¹£ad and which is described in Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. (I. 13, 5-7).

It is laid down that the {{sarskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} from {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}} to {{gÅ«dakarma|cÅ«á¸Äkarma}} were to be performed in the case of the twice-born classes with Vedic mantras when the child was a male and that in the case of girls the ceremonies were to be performed but without Vedic mantras; vide {{Asy, gs.|Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.}} (I. 15. 12, I. 16. 6, I. 17. 18), Manu II. 66, {{Yaj|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 13. The {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}20 from {{garbhadhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} to {{upenayana|upanayana}} alone were absolutely necessary in the case of all twice-born persons; the {{samskaras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} of snÄna and vivÄha were not obligatory, as a {{men|man}} was allowed to become a {{samnyÄsin|saá¹ƒnyÄsin}} (ascetic) immediately after finishing the period of studenthood (according to the {{Jabalo paniá¹£ad|JÄbÄlopaniá¹£ad}}). The {{SamskÄraprakasa|Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a}} enters upon an elaborate discussion as to whether {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}} could be performed for a child that is neither a male nor a female (pp. 195-197) and arrives at the conclusion that {{jÄtakarma|jÄtakarma}} and other {{samskaras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} cannot be performed for a {{kliba|klÄ«ba}} (impotent) child. [[198]]

Another question was as to what {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} could be performed for the {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}}. The view of {{VedavyÄgs|VedavyÄsa}} that he could have ten {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} performed (but without Vedic mantras) has been stated above (p. 159). The {{BaijavÄpa|BaijavÄpa}}21 {{gÄ¼hya|gá¹›hya}} says that seven {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} are allowed to the {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} viz. from {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} (or {{niá¹£eka|niá¹£eka}}) to caula. The view of AparÄrka22 appears to be that the eight {{sarÃ¶skÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} from {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} to caula (in {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 11-12) were meant for all vará¹‡as (including the {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}}). According to the Madanaratna, RÅ«panÄrÄyaá¹‡a and the bhÄá¹£ya of Harihara as quoted in the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu, the {{sÅ«dras|Å›Å«dras}} were entitled to perform six {{saá¹›skÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} viz. {{jatakarma|jÄtakarma}}, {{nÄmakarana|nÄmakaraá¹‡a}}, {{niá¹£kra maá¹‡a|niá¹£kramaá¹‡a}}, {{annaprÄÅ›ana|annaprÄÅ›ana}}, cÅ«á¸Ä and vivÄha and the five daily {{mahÄ. yajÃ±as|mahÄyajÃ±as}}. The {{SÅ«drakrtya-tattva|ÅšÅ«draká¹›tya-tattva}} of Raghunandana (p. 634) quotes a verse from the {{VarÄhapurÄá¹‡a|VarÄhapurÄá¹‡a}}23 ’this very procedure (about Å›rÄddha) has been declared in the case of {{sÅ«dras|Å›Å«dras}} but without mantras; for the {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} who is not entitled to repeat a mantra, a {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} repeats the mantra’ and then remarks that for a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} a mantra from the purÄá¹‡as is to be repeated by the {{brÄhmaá¹‡a|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} priest employed, that the {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}} is not to repeat even the purÄá¹‡a mantras but has only to say “namaá¸¥’. The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu24 mentions with approval the same view of {{ÅšÅ«lapÄá¹‡i|ÅšÅ«lapÄá¹‡i}} that in all religious ceremonies for {{sÅ«dras|Å›Å«dras}} the {{man. tras|mantras}} are to be {{takon|taken}} from the {{purÄpas|purÄá¹‡as}} and that they are to be repeated by the {{brÄhmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} priest. The BrahmapurÄá¹‡a25 quoted in the Sm. C. (I. p. 24) and other digests states that no other {{sarskÄra|saá¹ƒskÄra}} than vivÄha is allowed to the {{sÅ«drs|Å›Å«dra}}. On this the {{Nirpayasindhu|Nirá¹‡ayasindhu}} remarks that these conflicting views are to be {{reconoiled|reconciled}} by holding that the liberal ones apply to good (sat) {{sÅ«dras|Å›Å«dras}} and the stricter ones to low ({{ayat|asat}}) {{sÅ«dras|Å›Å«dras}} or that the rules are different in different countries. [[199]]

It is to be noted that in modern times most of the {{samg. kÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} (except {{garbbadhana|garbhadhÄna}}, upanayana and vivÄha) have fallen into oblivion and are {{bardly|hardly}} ever performed even by {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} in the manner and at the times prescribed by the smá¹›tis. Owing to the rapid rise in the marriageable age of {{brÄbmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} girls, even the saá¹ƒskÄra of {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} is falling into abeyance. {{NÄmakarana|NÄmakaraá¹‡a}}, {{annaprÄÅ›ana|annaprÄÅ›ana}} are performed in a popular way but without Vedic mantras or without calling a priest to officiate. In most cases caula is performed on the day of the {{upanayans|upanayana}} and samÄvartana is also performed a few days after upanayana. {{JÄtakarms|JÄtakarma}} and {{annaprÄbana|annaprÄÅ›ana}} are performed on the same day in some parts (e. g. in Bengal). It appears that this state of things has continued for centuries. The {{Smá¹›tyarthasÄra|Smá¹›tyarthasÄra}} (p. 3) says ‘if the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} (except upanayana) are not performed at the prescribed times, the {{VyÄhrti homa|VyÄhá¹›ti-homa}}26 should be offered and then the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} should be performed (though late). For each saá¹ƒskÄra that is not performed the penance called {{pÄdaká¹£cchra|pÄdaká¹›cchra}} should be performed (if the {{non performance|non-performance}} is due to some difficulty or distress), and for {{non performance|non-performance}} of caula the penance is {{ardha-kpoobra|ardhaká¹›cchra}}. If the {{sams kÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} were knowingly omitted or if there was no distress then the penance is double of this.’27 The Nirá¹‡ayasindhu quotes verses of {{Saunaka|Åšaunaka}}28 to this effect and then remarks that there was a conflict of views, some holding that after the penance the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} passed over should be performed all at one time, while others held that they should not be performed at all after undergoing penance and a third view was that if caula was left unperformed it may be performed on the same day as upanayana. The Dharmasindhu (3rd pariccheda, pÅ«rvÄrdha) states various substitutes (which are comparatively easy) for these penances. For example, one prÄjÄpatya penance is equal to three {{PÄdaksochras|PÄdaká¹›cchras}}. In place of prÄjÄpatya the person guilty of the lapse may make the gift of a cow or (in the absence of a cow) may give one niá¹£ka (320 guÃ±jÄs) of gold or one half or one-fourth of it; one who is very poor may give one-eighth of a silver niá¹£ka or corn of that value. There being these easy substitutes ({{pratyÄmnÄya|pratyÄmnÄya}} as they were called) people gradually left off performing the several {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} and concentrated themselves only on upanayana and vivÄha. The whole life of a person was so very minutely worked out and overlaid with so much ritual in the {{grhyasÄtras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}} and smá¹›tis that the tendency to neglect and change became insistent and inevitable. This tendency was helped by the accommodating spirit of the {{brÄhmana|brÄhmaá¹‡a}} authors of later smá¹›tis and digests that were ready to prescribe easier and easier substitutes for non-observance of the elaborate Sacraments, pre-natal and post-natal. HÄradatta in commenting on Gaut. I. 6 (upanayanam {{brÄhmaá¹‡asya aá¹£tame|brÄhmaá¹‡asyÄá¹£á¹­ame}}) remarks ’the teacher (Gautama) expounding upanayana first and passing over the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} like {{garbhadhÄns|garbhadhÄna}} that precede upanayana in time conveys that upanayana is the principal saá¹ƒskÄra. Therefore even if the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} like {{garbhÄdbÄna|garbhadhÄna}} did not take place owing to adverseness of fate, upanayana can be performed, but it follows that if upanayana be not performed there is no {{adhikÅ«ra|adhikÄra}} (eligibility) for marriage which follows only after upanayana.’29 In modern times in undergoing prÄyaÅ›citta for non-performance of the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} up to caula rupees two have to be paid to the priest (annas four for each of the {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} not performed up to caula and annas eight for caula).30 [[200]]

The {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} will now be described in detail. The material contained in the sÅ«tras, smá¹›tis and {{nibandhas|nibandhas}} is so vast that only very concise statements can be made here. The method followed will be as follows. Each saá¹ƒskÄra will be described from a few representative {{gÄ¼hya|gá¹›hya}} and dharma sÅ«tras such as the {{Ä€bv. gr., Ä€p. 8F.|Ä€Å›v. Gá¹›., Ä€p. Gá¹›.}}, and references will be given to other important works. Only important parts of procedure can be noted, minute divergences among the several works being passed over. Greater details will be given in the case of the two {{ohief|chief}} {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} viz, upanayana and marriage which are in vogue even now.31 [[201]]

{{GarbhadhÄna|GarbhadhÄna}}â€”The beginnings of this ceremony are found very early. Atharvaveda V. 25 appears to be a hymn intended for the {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} rite. Atharva V. 25. 3 and 5 are verses {{whiob|which}} {{ocour|occur}} in the Bá¹›. Up. VI. 4. 21; the passage of the Bá¹›. Up. VI. 4. 13, 19-22 may be rendered thus: ‘At the end of three days (after menstruation first appears) when she (wife) has bathed, the husband should make her pound rice (which is then boiled and eaten with various other things according as he desires a fair, brown or dark son or a learned son or a learned daughter)… and then towards morning, after having according to the rule of the {{Sthallpaka|SthÄlÄ«pÄka}} performed the preparation of the clarified butter, he sacrifices from the {{Sthalipaka|SthÄlÄ«pÄka}} little by little, saying ‘This is for Agni, svÄhÄ; this is for Anumati, svÄhÄ; this is for divine Savitá¹› the true creator, svÄhÄ!’ Having sacrificed he {{taken|takes}} out the rest of the {{sice|rice}}, eats it and after having eaten he gives some of it to his wife. Then he washes his hands, fills a water jar and sprinkles her thrice with water saying ‘Rise, oh {{ViÄvÄvasu|ViÅ›vÄvasu}}, seek another blooming girl, a wife with her husband.’ Then he embraces her and says ‘I am Ama, thou art SÄ. Thou art SÄ, I am Ama. I am the SÄman, thou art the á¹šk. I am the sky, thou art the earth. Come, let us strive together that a male child may be begotten’ (VI. 4. 21-22 cannot be literally translated for reasons of decency). Briefly the husband has intercourse with her and repeats certain mantras ‘may Viá¹£á¹‡u make ready your private parts, may Tvaá¹£á¹­Ä frame your beauty, may PrajÄpati sprinkle and may DhÄtÄ implant an embryo into you; Oh SinÄ«vÄlÄ«! Oh {{PÈ™thusá¹­ukÄ|Pá¹›thuá¹£á¹­ukÄ}}! implant embryo (in her), may the two AÅ›vins who wear a garland of lotuses plant in thee an embryo………. As the earth has fire inside it, as heaven has Indra inside it, as the wind is inside (as the embryo of) the quarters, so I plant a garbha in thee, oh, so and so (the name of the woman being taken)’.32 In the Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. (I. 13. 1) it is expressly stated that in the Upaniá¹£ad the ceremonies of {{Garbha lambbana|Garbhalambhana}} (conceiving a child), {{Pumsayana|Puá¹ƒsavana}} (securing a male child) and Anavalobhana (guarding against dangers to the embryo) are mentioned. Evidently this is a reference to the Bá¹›. Up. quoted above (where four mantras used in the {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} saá¹ƒskÄra by Hir. and other gá¹›hya sÅ«tras occur). [[202]]

Caturthikarma

The rite called {{caturthi karma|caturthÄ«karman}} is described in the {{Saá¹…khayana gr.|ÅšÄá¹…khÄyana-gá¹›hya}} (I. 18-19, S. B. E. vol. 29, pp. 44-46) as follows: “Three nights after marriage having elapsed, on the fourth the husband makes {{into|in the}} fire eight offerings of cooked food to Agni, VÄyu, SÅ«rya (the mantra being the same for all three except the name of the deity), Aryaman, Varuá¹‡a, PÅ«á¹£an (mantras being the same for these three), PrajÄpati (the mantra is á¹šg. X. 121. 10), to (Agni) Sviá¹£á¹­aká¹›t. Then he pounds the root of {{Adhyanda|Adhyaá¹‡á¸Ä}} plant and sprinkles it into the wife’s nostril with two verses (á¹šg. X. 85. 21-22) with svÄhÄ at the end of each. He should then touch her, when about to cohabit, with the words ’the mouth of the Gandharva {{VigvÄvasu|ViÅ›vÄvasu}} art thou’. Then he should murmur ‘into thy breath I put the sperm, Oh! so and so (the name of the wife)’ or he repeats the verse ‘as the earth has fire inside &c.’ (quoted above from Bá¹›. Up. VI. 4. 22) or several other verses in this strain ‘may a male embryo enter thy womb as an arrow into the quiver; may a man be born here, a son, after ten {{montbs|months}}’ “.33 The {{PÄr. gr.|PÄr. Gá¹›.}} (I. 11, S. B. E. vol. 29, pp. 288-290) also has a similar procedure. Ä€p. gá¹›. (8. 10-11, S. B. E. vol. 30, pp. 267-268), Gobhila II. 5 (S. B. E. vol. 30, pp. 51-52) give briefly a similar {{prooedure|procedure}}, but refer to mantras given in the MantrapÄá¹­ha (e. g. Ä€p. M. P. I. 10. 1. to I. 11. 11). To modern minds it appears strange that intercourse should have been surrounded by so much of mysticism and religion in the ancient sÅ«tras. But in ancient times every act was sought to be invested with a religious halo; so much so that according to Hir. gá¹›. I. 7. 25. 3. (S. B. E. vol. 30, p. 200) Ä€treya held that mantras were to be repeated at each cohabitation throughout life, while BÄdarÄyaá¹‡a prescribed that this was necessary only at the first cohabitation and after each monthly course.34 The Hir. gá¹›. (I. 7. 23. 11 to 7. 25. 8; S. B. E. vol. 30 pp. 197-200) gives a very elaborate rite, but on the same lines as the above {{grhyasÅ«tras|gá¹›hyasÅ«tras}}. One of the mantras is interesting on account of its reference to the cakravÄka birds (I. 7. 24. 6), “The concord that belongs to the {{oakrayaka|cakravÄka}} birds, that is brought out of the rivers of which the divine Gandharva is possessed, thereby we are concordant” (S. B. E. vol. 30, p. 198). The {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}} (III. 9) calls this ceremony {{ptusamgamana|á¹›tusaá¹ƒgamana}} and is similar to Ä€p. gá¹›. and Hir. gá¹›. It will be seen that the caturthÄ«karman is treated by the gá¹›hya writers as part of the marriage rites and the rite was performed irrespective of the question whether it was the first appearance of menses or whether the wife had just before the marriage come out of her monthly illness. This indicates that it was taken for granted that the wife had generally attained the age of puberty at the time of marriage. As the marriageable age of girls came down it appears that the rite of {{caturthikarma|caturthÄ«karman}} was discontinued and the rite was performed long after the ritual of marriage and appropriately named {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}. [[204]]

Garbhadhana

The smá¹›tis and {{nibandhas|nibandhas}} add many details some of which will have to be noticed. Manu (III. 46) and {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 79 say that the natural period (for conception) is sixteen nights from the appearance of menses. Ä€p. gá¹›. 9. 1 says35 that each of the even nights from the 4th to 16th (after the beginning of the monthly illness) are more and more suited for excellence of (male) offspring. {{HÄrlta|HÄrÄ«ta}} also says the same. These two appear to allow {{garbhidhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} on the fourth night, but Manu (III. 47), {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} (I. 79) lay down that the first four nights must be omitted. KÄtyÄyana, ParÄÅ›ara (VII. 17) and others say that a woman in her menses is purified by bathing on the 4th day. Laghu-Ä€Å›valÄyana (III. 1) says that the {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} ceremony should be performed on the first appearance of {{menges|menses}} after the 4th day has elapsed. The Sm. C. suggests that the 4th may be allowed if there is entire cessation of the flow.36 Manu (IV. 128) and {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 79 added further restrictions viz. that new moon and full moon days and the 8th and 14th tithis of the month were also to be omitted. Astrological details were added by {{YÄj|YÄjÃ±avalkya}}. 80 (that the MÅ«la and MaghÄ constellations must be avoided and the moon must be auspiciously placed) and other later smá¹›tis, which it is unnecessary to dwell upon. In the later smá¹›tis like Laghu-{{AsvalÄyana|Ä€Å›valÄyana}} III. 14-19 and in {{nibandhas|nibandhas}} like the Nirá¹‡ayasindhu and Dharmasindhu elaborate discussions are held about the months, tithis, week days, naká¹£atras, colour of clothes, that were deemed to be inauspicious for the first appearance of {{menges|menses}} and about the {{Å›Äntis|Å›Äntis}} (propitiatory rites) for averting their evil effects. Ä€p. gá¹›., Manu (III. 48), {{Yaj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} (I. 79), {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}} III. 9 hold that a man desirous of male issue should cohabit on the even days from the 4th day after the appearance of menses and if he cohabits on uneven days a female child is born. Hir. gá¹›.37 I. 7. 24. 8 (S. B. E. vol. 30 p. 199) and BhÄradvÄja gá¹›. (I. 20) prescribe that a woman in her menses who takes a bath on the 4th day should attire herself in white (or pure) clothes, should ornament herself and talk with (worthy) {{brÄhmaá¹‡as|brÄhmaá¹‡as}} (only). The {{Vaik.|Vaikh.}} (III. 9) further adds that she should anoint herself with unguents, should not converse with a woman, or a {{sÅ«dra|Å›Å«dra}}, should see no one else except her husband, since the child born becomes like the male whom a woman taking a bath after the period looks at. {{Saá¹…kba-Likhita|Åšaá¹…kha-Likhita}} convey a similar eugenic suggestion,38 viz. “Women give birth to a child similar in qualities to him on whomsoever their heart is set in their periods.’ [[205]]

A debatable question is whether {{garbhadhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} is a saá¹ƒskÄra of the garbha (the child in the womb) or of the woman. Gaut. VIII. 24, Manu II. 16, and {{YÄj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 10 indicate that it is a saá¹ƒskÄra of the garbha and not of the woman. ViÅ›varÅ«pa39 on {{Yaj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 11 expressly asserts that all {{samskÄras|saá¹ƒskÄras}} except {{Simantonnayana|SÄ«mantonnayana}} have to be performed again and again (as they are the saá¹ƒskÄras of the garbha), {{wbile|while}} {{Simantonnayana|SÄ«mantonnayana}} being a saá¹ƒskÄra of the woman has to be performed only once and this opinion was in consonance with the usage in his days. Laghu-{{AÅ›valdyana|Ä€Å›valÄyana}} (IV. 17) also holds the same view. MedhÄtithi40 on Manu II. 16 says that the {{garbhadhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} rite with mantras was performed after marriage only once at the time of the first cohabitation according to some, while according to others it was to be performed after every menstruation till conception. Later works like the Mit. (on {{Yaj.|YÄjÃ±avalkya}} I. 11), the Sm. C., the {{SamskÄratattva|Saá¹ƒskÄratattva}} (p. 909) hold that {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}}, {{pumsavada|puá¹ƒsavana}}41 and {{Bimantonnayana|SÄ«mantonnayana}} are saá¹ƒskÄras of the woman and are to be performed only once and quote HÄrÄ«ta in support. AparÄrka holds that {{slmantonnayana|sÄ«mantonnayana}} is performed only once at the first conception, while {{puinsavana|puá¹ƒsavana}} is repeated at each conception. He relies on PÄr. gá¹›. According to KullÅ«ka (on Manu II. 27), the Sm. C. (I. p. 14) and other works {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} is not of the nature of homa. The Dharmasindhu says that when {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} takes place on the first appearance of menses, homa for {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} is to be performed in the gá¹›hya fire, but there is no homa when the cohabitation takes place on the second or later appearance of menses; that those in whose sÅ«tra no homa is prescribed should perform the {{garbhÄdhÄna|garbhadhÄna}} rite on the proper day after the first appearance of menses by reciting the mantras but without homa. The {{Samskarakaustubha|Saá¹ƒskÄrakaustubha}} (p. 59) relying on {{Gshyapari sista|Gá¹›hyapariÅ›iá¹£á¹­a}} prescribes homa in which cooked food is to be offered to PrajÄpati and seven offerings of Äjya are to be offered in fire, three with the verses ‘{{Viá¹£á¹‡ur-yonim|Viá¹£á¹‡uryonim}}’ (á¹šg. We have here first the religious part of home and oblations with mantras. But this rite is mainly of a social and festival nature intended to keep the pregnant woman in good cheer. The SamskÄraprakÄÅ›a ( pp. 172-173 ) quotes some verses from an AsyalÄyana stating that this rite serves the purpose of driving certain female goblins that thirst for the destruction of the foetus; the parting of the hair by the husband with the quill, bunch of unripe fruits and darbhas and tying a garland round her neok, giving her boiled rice mixed with mudga and ghee and asking lute players to sing indicate its festive character. There is a great divergence among the gphya sutras about the several details of this samskÄra and the order in which they take place. The Sm. O. after pointing out a few divergences remarks that one should follow the rules of one’s own grbya sutra. A few important divergences are pointed out below. This samskÄra was to be performed in the 3rd month according to KÄthaka gr., in 3rd, 6th or 8th according to the MÄnava; in the 4th month according to AÅ›v., Ap., Hir. (7) Baud. gr. prescribes that the name may be derived froin a gage or a deity or an ancestor.558 The MÄnava grhya, how. ever, forbids the giving of a name of a deity itself, but allows the giving of a name derived from the name of a deity or a naká¹£a tra. Examples of names derived from sages would be VÄsistba, NÄrada &o, and of names taken from deities would be Viá¹£á¹‡u, Åšiva &o. The Mit, on YÄj. It should be noticed that in modern times most names in many parts of India are the names of deities or of heroes supposed to be avatÄras of deities. In Vedic literature hardly any human being bears the name of any of the Vedic gods (Indra, Mitra, PÅ«á¹£an &c). There are only a few exceptions such as that of Bbrgu ( in Tai. Up. The names that occur in the MahÄbhÄá¹£ya such as Devadatta, YajÃ±adatta, VÄyudatta (vol. II. p. 296 ), Viá¹£á¹‡umitra ( vol. Probably the practice began in the first centuries of the Christian era. From the fifth century onwards we have historio examples of such names, 6. g. in the Eran stone inscriptions of Budha gupta dated in the Gupta samvat 165 i. e. 484-5 A, D, (Gupta Inscriptions No. 19 ) there is a brÄhmaá¹‡a Indra-Viá¹£á¹‡u, son of Varuá¹‡a-Viá¹£á¹‡u, son of Hari-Viá¹£á¹‡u.

A few words may be said about matronymics. A few examples of such names have been given above from Vedic Literature. AÃ¸v. gr.574 (I. 5. 1.) says that in seleoting a bride groom or bride “one should first examine the family, as has been already said ’those who on the mother’s and father’s side’”. This refers to the AÅ›v. Srauta sÅ«tra where it is required that both parents of the brÄhmaá¹‡a at the time of camasabhaká¹£aá¹‡a in DaÅ›apoya should be for ten generations perfect in their learning, austerities, and meritorious works and who can be traced to have throughout been of the brÄhmaá¹‡a class on both sides &o. YÄj. I. 54 enjoins that one should choose a girl from

& great family of Å›rotriyas, which has been famous for ten generations (for learning and character ). Therefore when in certain cases a person is named after his mother or after the gotra of his mother’s father, all that is intended to be conveyed is that he is descended from worthy male and female ancestors. There is no question in such cases of matriarchy. In the Nasik Inscription No. 2 (E. I. vol. VIII. p. 60) siri PuÄ¼umÄyi is des cribed as VÄsithiputa and in E. I. vol. In all these cases the mother’s gotra name is specially emphasized probably to convey that the mothers were of the bluest blood. Some faint light is thrown on this matter by the ArthasÄstra of Kautilya, 81% which says that the prince after the performance of caula is to engage in the study of the alphabet and of arithmetio, and after his upanayana he is to study the Vedas, Å«nviká¹£iki (metaphysics ), vÄrlÄ ( agriculture and the science of wealth ) and dandaniti (the art of government) up till the 16th year when the godÄna ceremony is to be performed and after which year ho may marry. Bana has its probably the ArthasÄstra in view when he makes prince CandrÄpida enter the temple of learning ( vidyÄ mandira ) at 6 and remain there till he became sixteen and he (like Milton in his letter to Hartlile ) tells us how extensive the ideal curriculum of studies in arts and soiences for the prince was thought to be. In the Uttararamacarita ( Act II) it is said that Kusa and Lata were taught vidyÄs other than the Veda after their caula and before upanayana.

It appears that at least from the early centuries of the Christian era, & ceremony called VidyÄrambha (commencement of learning the alphabet ) was celebrated. Apararka (pp. 30-31) and the Sm.C.(I. p.26)cite verses from the Markandeya-puranasit

SanskÄras-VidyÄrambha

about vidyÄrambha as follows: in the fifth year of the child on some day from the 12th of the bright half of Kartika to the 11th of the bright half of Aá¹£adha, but excluding the 18t, 6th, 8th, 15th tithi or rikta tithis (i. e. 4th, 9th and 14th ) and Saturday and Tuesday, the ceremony of beginning to learn should be performed. Having worshipped Hari ( Viá¹£á¹‡u ), Laká¹£mi, Sarasvati, the sutra writers of one’s sÄkha and the lore peculiar to one’s family, one should offer in the fire oblations of olarified butter to the above mentioned deities and should hone our brÄhmaá¹‡as by the payment of daká¹£iá¹‡Ä. The teacher should sit facing the east and the boy should face the west and the teacher should begin to teach the first lesson to the boy who should receive the benedictions of brÄhmanas. Thereafter teaching should be stopped on the days of anadhyÄya (which will be specified later on).

The SamskÄra-prakÄÅ›a (pp. 321-325 ) and SamskÄraratna inÄlÄ (pp. 904-907 ) have an extensivo note, a considerable part of which is devoted to astrological matters. Puá¹ƒsavanaâ€”This rite is so called because in virtue of it a male is born.47 The word ‘{{puinsuvana|puá¹ƒsavana}}’ occurs in the Atharvaveda VI. 11. 1 where48 it appears to be used literally (in the sense of ‘giving birth to a male child’) ‘The AÅ›vattha tree is on top of the ÅšamÄ« tree, there the birth of a male has been effected’. Puá¹ƒsavana

There is some difference of view as to details. The Ä€p. gá¹›., Hir. gá¹›. and BhÄradvÄja gá¹›. place {{pumgavana|puá¹ƒsavana}} after sÄ«mantonnayana and Ä€p. says it may be performed when pregnancy becomes apparent. Instead of two beans and a barley grain in curds, he requires the bringing of a shoot of the branch of a nyagrodha tree which (tree) points eastward or northward and which has two (fruits that look like) testicles and the pounding of the shoot and fruits by a girl who has not attained puberty between two upper stones of (two mills) with water. He then prescribes that the wife should lie down on her back to the west of the fire herself facing the east and that the husband should insert in her right nostril the pounded substance with his thumb with the formula (puá¹ƒsavanam-asi) found in Ä€p. M. P. (II. 11. 14).50 {{San. gr.|ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›.}} (1. 20, S. B. E. vol. 29, p. 46) says that the rite may be performed on Tiá¹£ya or ÅšrÄvaá¹‡a, while the BhÄradvÄja gá¹›. Ä€Å›v., Hir., {{San.|ÅšÄá¹….}}, Gobhila, KhÄdira prescribe the third month. YÄj. I. 11, PÄr. gá¹›. (I. 14), Viá¹£á¹‡u Dh. S. 27. 2 and Bá¹›haspati (quoted in the Sm. DevapÄla (com. of KÄá¹­haka gá¹›.) says the usage [[219]] of Å›iá¹£á¹­as is to perform it in the 8th month and Brahmabala (another com.) says that the usage is to perform it in the 7th or 8th month of pregnancy. Most of the gá¹›hyasÅ«tras refer to the pounding of the shoot52 of the Nyagrodha tree (or some other plant) and inserting the pounded substance in the wife’s right nostril. The mantras repeated when inserting the substance in her nostril are different according to most sÅ«tras. ÅšÄá¹…khÄyana (S. B. E. vol. 29, p. 46) prescribes the four verses á¹šg. [[220]]

It would be clear that the puá¹ƒsavana rite has several elements, religious (homa and son’s importance from ancient times), symbolical or suggestive (the drinking of curds with beans and grain of barley) and medical (inserting some substance in the woman’s nostrils). For what purpose the placing of the bile of a tortoise on the woman’s lap was prescribed by PÄraskara alone (I. 14) it is difficult to say.

Later works like the {{SamskÄraratnamÄlÄ|Saá¹ƒskÄraratnamÄlÄ}} prescribe a homa for puá¹ƒsavana also and remark that when in the absence of the husband, the husband’s brother or other relative performs the rite, it should be performed in ordinary kitchen fire (p. 815). This latter rule applies to SÄ«mantonnayana also.

Anavalobhana or {{Garbharaká¹£ama|Garbharaká¹£aá¹‡a}}â€”This rite was apparently part of Puá¹ƒsavana. Ä€Å›v. gá¹›., as already said, separately mentions puá¹ƒsavana and anavalobhana as referred to in the Upaniá¹£ad. The BaijavÄpa gá¹›. (quoted in the {{SamskÄrapra kaÅ›a|Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a}} p. 171) says ‘He performs the puá¹ƒsavana and anavalobhana in the fortnight of the waxing moon on an auspicious day when the moon is in conjunction with a naká¹£atra (deemed to be) a male’. This shows that both were performed on the same day. Another sÅ«tra of BaijavÄpa quoted in the {{SamskÄramayÅ«kha|Saá¹ƒskÄramayÅ«kha}}53 says that the two are to be performed in the 2nd or 3rd month of pregnancy.

Anavalobhana

This rite is so called because by virtue of its performance the foetus does not fall out or is not destroyed (i. e. it is really anavalopana from the root lup with ava). Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. Having offered a sacrifice of cooked food to PrajÄpati he should touch the region of her heart with the verse ‘Oh thou whose hair is well parted, what is hidden in thy heart, in PrajÄpati, that I understand, (mayst thou understand) me who know that; may not injury to the son be my lot’. [[221]]

It will be seen that the inserting of dÅ«rvÄrasa in the woman’s nostril, touching her heart and prayers to the gods for the safety of the foetus are the principal features of this rite in Ä€Å›v.

According to Åšaunaka-kÄrikÄ (Ms. in Bombay University Library, folio 138) that rite is called anavalobhana whereby the foetus remains undisturbed or does not fall out.55 According to the Smá¹›tyarthasÄra it is to be performed in the fourth month. According to Laghu-Ä€Å›valÄyana IV. 1-2 anavalobhana and sÄ«mantonnayana were to be performed in the 4th, 6th or 8th month of pregnancy and verses 6-7 give the same details as in Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.

The {{San. gr.|ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›.}} (I. 21. 1-3, S. B. This seems to be another version of anavalobhana. [[222]]

According to Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. kÄrikÄs of KumÄrila (I. 6. 5) this rite is to be repeated on every conception. Most other writers would hold that like puá¹ƒsavana it is to be performed only once.

SÄ«mantonnayanaâ€”This is treated in the following gá¹›. sÅ«tras: Ä€Å›v. I. 14. 1-9; ÅšÄá¹…. I. 22, Ä€p. 14. 1-8, Hir. II. 1, Baudh. I. 10, BhÄradvÄja I. 21, Gobhila II. 7. 1-12, KhÄdira II. 2. 24-28, PÄr. I. 15, KÄá¹­haka 31. 1-5, VaikhÄnasa III. 12.

In the Ä€p. mantrapatha thirteen verses are devoted to this saá¹ƒskÄra in all, some of which occur in the á¹šg., the Atharvaveda and Tai. S.

SÄ«mantonnayana

We have here first the religious part of homa and oblations with mantras. But this rite is mainly of a social and festival nature intended to keep the pregnant woman in good cheer. The {{SamskÄraprakÄÅ›a|Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a}} (pp. 172-173) quotes some verses from an Ä€Å›valÄyana stating that this rite serves the purpose of driving certain female goblins that thirst for the destruction of the foetus; the parting of the hair by the husband with the quill, bunch of unripe fruits and darbhas and tying a garland round her neck, giving her boiled rice mixed with mudga and ghee and asking lute players to sing indicate its festive character. There is a great divergence among the gá¹›hya sÅ«tras about the several details of this saá¹ƒskÄra and the order in which they take place. The Sm. C. after pointing out a few divergences remarks that one should follow the rules of one’s own gá¹›hya sÅ«tra. A few important divergences are pointed out below. This saá¹ƒskÄra was to be performed in the 3rd month according to KÄá¹­haka gá¹›., in 3rd, 6th or 8th according to the MÄnava; in the 4th month according to Ä€Å›v., Ä€p., Hir. (27. 3), Åšaá¹…kha; in the 7th according to ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›. (I. 22. 1), in the 8th according to Vaik. and Veda-VyÄsa (I. 18). Åšaá¹…kha as quoted in the Smá¹›ticandrikÄ (I. p. 17) says that it should be performed on the foetus beginning to move and up to the time of delivery. Ä€Å›v., ÅšÄá¹…. and Hir. require that the moon must be in conjunction with a male naká¹£atra. Hir. gá¹›. alone prescribes that the saá¹ƒskÄra should take place in a round apartment. It is remarkable that Ä€Å›v. alone requires that the woman should be seated on a bull’s hide, which shows that till his day such a hide was not treated (as is done in modern times) as a very unholy thing. PÄraskara makes her sit on a soft chair or seat, while Gobhila prescribes a seat of northward pointed darbhas. There is great divergence as to the number of oblations and the verses to be repeated even in sÅ«tras of the same Veda, e. g. ÅšÄá¹…. says that the unripe fruits are to be tied to a string of three twisted threads and the string is to be suspended from her neck as a garland. PÄr. also seems to suggest the same. Ä€Å›v., Hir., Baudh., BhÄradvÄja and PÄr. prescribe a gÄthÄ in honour of king Soma (the plant). For the difference of view among {{nibandhakÄras|nibandhakÄras}} vide above (pp. 205-206) on {{garbhÄdbÄna|garbhadhÄna}}. [[226]]

Viá¹£á¹‡u was of opinion that sÄ«mantonnayana is a saá¹ƒskÄra of the woman, but that according to some it is a saá¹ƒskÄra of the foetus and so was to be repeated at each conception.60 On account of the great divergence of details one may conjecture that this saá¹ƒskÄra was not very ancient in the times of the Gá¹›hyasÅ«tras.

Soá¹£yantikarmaâ€”Vide Ä€p. gá¹›. 14. 13-15; Hir. gá¹›. II. 2. 8-II. 3. 1, BhÄradvÄja gá¹›. I. 22, Gobhila gá¹›. II. 7. 13-14, KhÄdira gá¹›. II. 2. 29-30, PÄr. gá¹›. I. 16, KÄá¹­haka gá¹›. 33. 1-3. JÄtakarmaâ€”This appears to have been a rite of hoary antiquity. In the Tai. JÄtakarma

It will be clear from the above passages of the Bá¹›. Up. that the jÄtakarma rite contained the following parts: (1) homa of curds with ghá¹›ta to the accompaniment of mantras; (2) repeating in the child’s right ear the word ‘speech’ thrice; (3) making the child lick curds, honey and ghá¹›ta by means of a golden ladle (or ring); (4) addressing the child with a name which was to be his secret name (nÄmakaraá¹‡a); (5) putting the child to the breast; (6) addressing the mother with mantras. The Åšatapatha adds another detail viz. asking five brÄhmaá¹‡as if available to breathe on the child (from four quarters, east, south, west, north and one immediately above him) or the father himself may do so. [[231]]

There is great divergence in the gá¹›hyasÅ«tras on the different details that go to make up the jÄtakarma. Some give almost all the above seven details, while others omit some of them. The order of these components differs in the gá¹›hya sÅ«tras and according to the Veda to which each sÅ«tra is attached the mantras differ. It would be impossible to give in a brief compass the details from all gá¹›hyasÅ«tras. Some description, however, of the details from important gá¹›hyasÅ«tras is given below:

The ceremony has to be performed by the very necessities of the case immediately after birth. But different sÅ«tras express it in different ways, e. g. Ä€Å›v. I. 15. 2 says the rite should be done before any other person (than the mother and nurse) touches the child. PÄr. gá¹›. (I. 16) says it is performed before the navel string is cut off. Gobhila (II. 7. 17) and KhÄdira II. 2. 32 say that it is to be performed before the navel string is cut off and the breast is given to the child.

In the Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. (I. 15. 1-4) the ceremony is described as follows: “When a son has been born, he (the father) should before other persons touch him, give to the child to eat honey and clarified butter in which gold has been rubbed by means of a golden (spoon) with the verse ‘I give unto thee the Veda (wisdom or knowledge) of honey and ghá¹›ta, (Veda) which is produced by the god Savitá¹› (who urges on) the bountiful; may you have long life and may you live in this world for a hundred autumns being protected by the gods’. Bringing near the child’s two ears (his mouth) the father mutters {{medhajanana|medhÄjanana}}68 ‘may god Savitá¹› bestow on thee intelligence; may the goddess SarasvatÄ« bestow on thee intelligence and may the two gods AÅ›vins wearing wreaths of lotus give to thee intelligence’.69 He touches the (son’s) two shoulders (with the mantra) ‘be a stone, be an axe, be indestructible gold; thou art indeed Veda, called son; so live a hundred autumns’ and (with the mantra) ‘Oh Indra, bestow the best wealth’ (á¹šg. II. 21. 6) and ‘Oh Maghavan (bountiful Indra)!70 Oh (Indra) partaker of á¹›jÄ«á¹£a! bestow on us’ (á¹šg. III. 36. 10). And let them give him a name”. The following sÅ«tras (I. 15. 5-10) lay down rules about the name, which will be considered under NÄmakaraá¹‡a. [[232]]

It will be noticed that out of the several components of the rite described in the Bá¹›. Up. and the Åšat. Br., Ä€Å›v. omits express mention of homa, of putting the child to the breast (stanadÄna), the address to the mother (mÄtrÄbhimantraá¹‡a), and the breathing over the child by five brÄhmaá¹‡as or the father. The ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›. (I. 24. 1-12) also omits homa, the stanadÄna and mÄtrÄbhimantraá¹‡a, but refers to the father breathing over the new born child thrice. Instead of ghá¹›ta and honey served with a golden spoon, ÅšÄá¹…. prescribes mixing of curds, honey, ghá¹›ta and water or grinding together of rice and barley. It adds the tying of gold to a hempen string and fixing it on the right hand of the child till the mother gets up from child-bed.

It will have been noticed that Ä€Å›v. and ÅšÄá¹…. both prescribe giving a secret name to the child on the day of birth and do not prescribe a separate NÄmakaraá¹‡a ceremony. ÅšÄá¹…. gá¹›. (I. 24. 6) adds that a vyÄvahÄrika name may be given on the 10th day from birth. The Gobhila gá¹›. (II. 7. 13-15) and KhÄdira gá¹›. II. 2. 28-31 say that a secret name for the child that is to be born is to be uttered in the Soá¹£yanti-karma. So Ä€Å›v. probably carries on that tradition.

We shall now take the several components of the rite and show how they are dealt with by the several gá¹›hyasÅ«tras.

(10) Keeping off evil spiritsâ€”Though Ä€Å›v. and ÅšÄá¹…. are entirely silent on this point several sÅ«tras devote large space to this topic and are full of mantras which are more or less magic. It would not be out of place to mention a few other subsidiary matters. Baudh., Ä€p., Hir. and Vaik. expressly say that the boy is to have a bath. The Hir. and Vaik.76 say that the axe is to be placed on a stone and gold is to be placed on the axe, then these are to be turned upside down (so that gold lies at the bottom and the stone is on top) and then the boy is to be held head eastwards above the stone by a female in her two hands, while the father repeats the two mantras “be a stone &c.” and the mantra ’thou art produced from (my) limb by limb &c.’ This shows how what was once only symbolical (viz. uttering the mantras “be a stone’ &c.” indicating the desire that the boy should be strong, sharp and worthy like a stone, axe and gold) became transformed into a rite requiring physical presence of these things. PÄr., Ä€p., Hir., BhÄr. and Vaik. prescribe that a pot full of water should be placed towards the head (of the woman and her child) with a mantra ‘Oh waters! [[236]] watch while (people are asleep)’. None of the sÅ«tras (except VaikhÄnasa) refers to any astrological details. Vaik. (III. 14) says that when the child’s nose appears, the position of the planets should be observed and his future welfare or otherwise should be examined, since the boy is to be so brought up as to enhance his good qualities. Both Ä€p. and Baudh. say that the remnants of honey, curds and ghá¹›ta should be mixed with water and poured out in a cowstable (and not thrown about in an impure place). This ceremony is comparatively brief in Ä€p., ÅšÄá¹…. and a few others, but in Hir., PÄr. and BhÄradvÄja it is most elaborate and would require an unduly long time considering the state of the newly born child and the woman in child-bed. There is no wonder, therefore, that this ceremony gradually went out of vogue. In modern times a few well-to-do families in Western India sometimes perform what is called ‘putrÄvana’ (in Marathi) and make the boy lick honey and ghá¹›ta by means of a golden piece or ring. The dangers to the child of an elaborate ritual must have been apparent to all people even in ancient times.77

The Sm. C. In modern times on the 5th and 6th days after birth certain ceremonies are performed for which there is no warrant in the sÅ«tras. There is great divergence of view as to the time when the child was named. Several times are suggested in the ancient literature and in the sÅ«tras and smá¹›tis.

(a) We have already seen (p. 232) that a child was addressed by a name, according to Gobhila and KhÄdira, even in the Soá¹£yantikarma.

In á¹šg. The principal rules about names may now be set out from the gá¹›hyasÅ«tras. Ä€Å›v. (I. 15. 4-10, S. B. It should be noticed that Ä€Å›v. and ÅšÄá¹…. differ on one very important point. According to Ä€Å›v. the name for which elaborate rules are laid down is to be the ordinary name and he lays down no rule about the formation of the secret name; while ÅšÄá¹…. lays down for the secret name the same rules as Ä€Å›v. does for the public one and ÅšÄá¹…. says about the public name that it should be pleasing.

Instead of quoting gá¹›hya sÅ«tras at length the principal rules about names deducible from them may be stated in the form of propositions with a few illustrations for each.

(1) The first rule in almost all sÅ«tras is that the name for males should contain two or four syllables or an even number. This rule is deduced from Vedic literature where most of the names contain either two syllables (e. g. Baka, Trita, Kutsa, Bhá¹›gu) or four syllables (Trasadasyu, Purukutsa, MedhyÄtithi, Brahmadatta &c.), though names of three syllables (like Kavaá¹£a, Cyavana, Bharata) and of five syllables (like NÄbhÄnediá¹£á¹­ha, Hiraá¹‡yastÅ«pa) are not wanting. BaijavÄpa gá¹›hya allowed the name to be of one, two, three, four or any number of syllables. ÅšÄá¹…. allowed a name even of six syllables and Baudh. gá¹›. (II. 1. 25) of six or even eight. Examples of names with two syllables and four syllables are given below.96

(2) Almost all gá¹›hya sÅ«tras contain the rule that the name should begin with a sonant and contain in the middle a semivowel. This is stated also in the ancient quotation from YÄjÃ±ikas in the MahÄbhÄá¹£ya.

(3) Some sÅ«tras prescribe that the name should end in a visarga preceded97 by a long vowel (e. g. Ä€p., BhÄr., Hir., PÄr.). Ä€Å›v. only mentions that it should end in a visarga, while Vaik. and Gobhila say that it may end in a long vowel or in a visarga.

These rules were probably based on such Vedic names as SudÄs, DÄ«rghatamas, Pá¹›thuÅ›ravas (that occur in the á¹šgveda) and such names as VatsaprÄ« (Tai. S. V. 2. 1. 6).

(4) Ä€p. prescribes that the name should have two parts, the first being a noun and the second a verbal formation (generally a past passive participle). This rule is probably based on such ancient names as Brahmadatta (which occurs in Bá¹›. Up. I. 3. 24 and figures very much in Pali works), Devadatta, YajÃ±adatta &c.

(5) Many gá¹›hya sÅ«tras (like PÄr., Gobhila, ÅšÄá¹…., BaijavÄpa, VÄrÄha) say that the name should be formed from a root by a ká¹›t affix and should not be a taddhita (i. e. formed from a noun by an affix).

(6) Ä€p. and Hir. say that the name should have the upasarga ‘su’ in it as a BrÄhmaá¹‡a passage says that such a name has stability in it. Examples are SujÄta, SudarÅ›ana, SukeÅ›as (PraÅ›na Up. I. 1).

(7) Baudh. gá¹›. prescribes that the name may be derived from a sage or a deity or an ancestor.98 The MÄnava gá¹›hya, however, forbids the giving of a name of a deity itself, but allows the giving of a name derived from the name of a deity or a naká¹£atra. Examples of names derived from sages would be VÄsiá¹£á¹­ha, NÄrada &c. and of names taken from deities would be Viá¹£á¹‡u, Åšiva &c. The Mit. on YÄj. It should be noticed that in modern times most names in many parts of India are the names of deities or of heroes supposed to be avatÄras of deities. In Vedic literature hardly any human being bears the name of any of the Vedic gods (Indra, Mitra, PÅ«á¹£an &c). There are only a few exceptions such as that of Bhá¹›gu (in Tai. Up. But in the Vedic Literature persons have names derived from the names of gods, such as Indrota (Indra + Å«ta, protected), Indradyumna. The names that occur in the MahÄbhÄá¹£ya such as Devadatta, YajÃ±adatta, VÄyudatta (vol. II. p. 296), Viá¹£á¹‡umitra (vol. From the fifth century onwards we have historic examples of such names, e. g. in the Eraá¹‡ stone inscriptions of Budhagupta dated in the Gupta saá¹ƒvat 165 i. e. 484-5 A. D. (8) Baudh., PÄr., Gobhila (and the YÄjÃ±ikas quoted by the MahÄbhÄá¹£ya) prescribe that the name of the boy may be the same as that of any of the ancestors of the father. The MÄnava gá¹›. (9) The gá¹›hyasÅ«tras (except PÄr. and MÄnava) are agreed that a secret name is to be given to the boy by the parents, in the Soá¹£yantikarma according to Gobhila and KhÄdira, at birth according to some (like Ä€Å›v. and KÄá¹­haka) and according to others (like Ä€p., Baudh., BhÄr.) at the time of NÄmakaraá¹‡a on 10th or 12th day. The naká¹£atranÄma was of importance in the performance of Vedic sacrifices. Hundreds of names occur in the Vedic Literature, but there are hardly any names directly derived from a naká¹£atra. Matronymics

A few words may be said about matronymics. A few examples of such names have been given above from Vedic Literature. Ä€Å›v. gá¹›.114 (I. 5. 1) says that in selecting a bridegroom or bride “one should first examine the family, as has been already said ’those who on the mother’s and father’s side’”. This refers to the Ä€Å›v. YÄj. I. 54 enjoins that one should choose a girl from a great family of Å›rotriyas, which has been famous for ten generations (for learning and character). Therefore when in certain cases a person is named after his mother or after the gotra of his mother’s father, all that is intended to be conveyed is that he is descended from worthy male and female ancestors. There is no question in such cases of matriarchy. In the NÄsik Inscription No. 2 (E. I. vol. VIII, p. 60) Siri Puá¸·umÄyi is described as VÄsiá¹­hÄ«puta and in E. I. vol. In all these cases the mother’s gotra name is specially emphasized probably to convey that the mothers were of the bluest blood. Comparatively late writers mention the gotra in which their mother was born (e. g. BhavabhÅ«ti who flourished about 700-750 A. D. says that he was a KÄÅ›yapa while his mother was a JÄtÅ«kará¹‡Ä«). From a KÄrikÄ in the MahÄbhÄá¹£ya we learn that the great grammarian PÄá¹‡ini was the son of a DÄká¹£Ä«. PÄá¹‡ini himself116 (IV. 1. 147) delivers a [[253]] special rule about the formation of a name for a man from the gotra name of his mother to convey contempt (e. g. GÄrgaá¸¥ or GÄrgikaá¸¥, a rogue, from his mother’s name GÄrgÄ«).117 ÅšÄá¹…khÄyana gá¹›hya (I. 25. 2-9, S. B. NÄmakaraá¹‡a

The Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. does not describe NÄmakaraá¹‡a. The later works state many details which it is unnecessary to set out. The mother with her child on her lap sits to the right of the father. Some late writers prescribe that the father is to give a secret name to the boy and should spread husked grains of rice in a vessel of bronze, write thereon with a golden pen the words ‘salutations to ÅšrÄ« Gaá¹‡apati’ and then write four names of the boy, viz. kuladevatÄ-nÄma (such as YogeÅ›varÄ«-bhakta), then mÄsanÄma (vide note 566 above), a vyÄvahÄrikanÄma, and a naká¹£atranÄma.119

Some sÅ«tra works add a detail immediately after NÄmakaraá¹‡a. For example, Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. Ä€p. gá¹›. 15. 12120 prescribes that on returning from a journey the father should address his son (abhimantraá¹‡a) with the verse ‘aá¹…gÄd,’ should smell the child on the head with the verse ‘be thou an axe’ and should mutter in his right ear five mantras. These rules have a very ancient origin. The Kauá¹£Ä«taki Br. Up. In the case of the girl there is no smelling of the head nor muttering in the ear, but only address (with a prose formula). This no doubt indicates that greater value was attached to a son than to a daughter, but it also shows that the daughter was not altogether neglected.

Kará¹‡avedhaâ€”(piercing the lobes of the ears of the child). In modern times this is generally done on the 12th day after birth. In the Baudh. gá¹›. The Sm. C. has a brief note on kará¹‡avedha. The gá¹›hya-pariÅ›iá¹£á¹­a says that the father sits facing the east in the first half of the day and first addresses the right ear of the boy with the mantra ‘Oh gods, may we hear bliss with our ears’ (á¹šg. In modern times, generally a goldsmith is called who pierces the lower lobes of the ears with a pointed golden wire and turns it into a ring round the lobes. In the case of girls the left ear is pierced first. That ears of boys were pierced even in ancient times is suggested by a mantra quoted in the Nirukta.122 ‘He (the teacher) who pierces the ear with truth, without causing pain and yet bestowing ambrosia, should be regarded as one’s father and mother’.

Kará¹‡avedha

Niá¹£kramaá¹‡aâ€”(Taking the child out of the house into the open). This is a minor rite. PÄr. gá¹›. I. 17 gives the briefest description. Vide also Gobhila II. 8. 1-7 (S. B. According to PÄr. gá¹›. the father makes the child124 look at the sun pronouncing the verse ’that eye’ (VÄj. S. 36. 24). Baudh. [[256]] gá¹›. (II. 2) prescribes a homa with eight oblations. Gobhila speaks of candradarÅ›ana. It says that on the 3rd tithi of the third bright fortnight after birth, the father bathes the child in the morning, worships in the evening the moon with folded hands, then the mother, having dressed the child, hands it with its face to the north from the south to north to the father and herself passes behind the back of the father and stands to the north of him, who worships with the three verses125 ‘Oh thou whose hair is well parted, thy heart’ (Mantra-brÄhmaá¹‡a I. 5. 10-12), then the father hands back the son to the mother with the words ’that this son may not come to harm and be torn from his mother’. The KhÄdira-gá¹›hya has practically the same rules, except that it does not speak of two times. It will be noted that both omit the sight of the sun, but only mention the seeing of the moon. Laghu-Ä€Å›valÄyana VII. 1-3 speaks of the performance of abhyudayika Å›rÄddha, then reciting the sÅ«kta from ‘svasti no mimitÄm’ (á¹šg. V. 51. 11) and ‘ÄÅ›u Å›iÅ›Änaá¸¥’ (á¹šg. Vará¹£avardhana or abdapÅ«rtiâ€”In some of the sÅ«tras provision is made for some ceremonies every month on the day of the birth of the child for one year and on every anniversary of the day of birth throughout life.
