Caula

Caula or CÅ«á¸Äkarma or CÅ«á¸Äkaraá¹‡aâ€”(the first cutting of the hair on the child’s head). This saá¹ƒskÄra is mentioned by every writer. ‘CÅ«á¸Ä’ means the ’lock or tuft of hair kept on the head when the remaining part is shaved (i. e. the Å›ikhÄ); so cÅ«á¸Äkarma or cÅ«á¸Äkaraá¹‡a means that rite in which a lock of hair is kept (for the first time after birth). We get ‘caula’ from ‘cÅ«á¸Ä’136 meaning ‘a rite the purpose of which is keeping a lock of hair’ and ‘á¸a’ and ’la’ often interchange places. So we get ‘caula’ or ‘caula’ also as the name of the ceremony.

According to many137 writers caula was performed in the third year from birth. Baudh. gá¹›. (II. 4), PÄr. gá¹›. (II. 1), Manu II. 35, Vaik. III. 23 say that it may be performed in the 1st or 3rd year. Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. and VÄrÄha gá¹›. say it may be performed in the 3rd year or in the year in which it is the custom of the family to perform it. PÄr. also refers to family usage. YÄj. specifies no year, but mentions only family usage. Yama quoted by AparÄrka (p. 29) allowed it in the first, 2nd or 3rd year, while Åšaá¹…kha-Likhita allowed it in the 3rd or 5th (AparÄrka p. 29). á¹¢aá¸-guru-Å›iá¹£ya quoted in the Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a (p. 296) and NÄrÄyaá¹‡a (on Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. I. 17. 1) say that some performed it at the time of upanayana. [[261]]

Whether such a ceremony was performed in the Vedic ages cannot be ascertained with certainty. BhÄr. gá¹›. I. 28 expressly138 refers to the Vedic verse (á¹šg. IV. 75. 17 or Tai. S. IV. 6. 4. 5) as indicative of the practice of Caula in Vedic times ‘where arrows fall together like boys having many tufts of hair’. Manu II. 35 also has in view this Vedic verse.

The principal act in this ceremony is the cutting of the hair of the child. The other subsidiary matters are the performance of homa, feeding of brÄhmaá¹‡as, receiving of their benedictions and giving of daká¹£iá¹‡Ä, the disposal of cut hair in such a way that no one can find them.

The ceremony is to be performed on an auspicious day as set out in note 494 above. Ä€p. gá¹›. 16. 3 says it should be performed when the moon is in conjunction with Punarvasu naká¹£atra, while MÄnava gá¹›. says that it should not be done on the 9th tithi of a month. Later works like the Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a (pp. 299-315) give very intricate rules about the auspicious times, which rules are passed over here. The most exhaustive treatment of this ceremony in the sÅ«tra works is to be found in Ä€Å›v., Gobhila, VÄrÄha and PÄr. II. 1.

The materials required in this ceremony are stated as follows: (1) To the north of the fire are placed four vessels each of which is separately filled with rice, barley, mÄá¹£a beans and sesame respectively (Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. I. 17. 2), but Gobhila (II. 9. 6-7) says that they are to be placed to the east and Gobhila and ÅšÄá¹…. say that these are to be given to the barber at the end of the rite; (2) to the west of the fire the mother with the boy on her lap is to be seated and two vessels one filled with the dung of a bull and the other with Å›amÄ« leaves are to be also placed to the west (Gobhila II. 9. 5 and KhÄdira II. 3. 18 place the dung to the north of the fire and KhÄdira says that she sits to the north); (3) to the right of the mother the father sits holding 21 bunches139 of kuÅ›a grass or the brahmÄ priest (if there be any) may hold them; (4) warm and cold water or only warm water; (5) an ordinary razor or one made of Udumbara wood (according to KhÄdira II. 3. 17 and Gobhila II. 9. 4); (6) a [[262]] mirror (Gobhila and KhÄdira). According to Gobhila and KhÄdira the barber, hot water, mirror, razor and bunches of kuÅ›a grass are to the south of the fire and bull’s dung and a mess of rice mixed with sesame are to the north of the fire. Ä€Å›v., PÄr., KÄá¹­haka and MÄnava say that the razor is to be of loha (which the commentator NÄrÄyaá¹‡a explains as copper).

After homa is performed, the principal matter (of cutting the hair) is to be begun. According to Gobhila and KhÄdira the father, having contemplated upon Savitá¹›, looks at the barber with the mantra ‘here comes Savitá¹›’ (Mantra-br. I. 6. 1) and contemplating on VÄyu looks at the warm water with the mantra ‘with warm water, Oh VÄyu, come hither’ (Mantra-Br. I. 6. 2). The father then mixes the hot and cold water and may put, in a part of the water, butter or drops of curds and apply the water to moisten three times the boy’s head with the mantra ‘may Aditi cut thy hair; may the waters moisten (thy hair) for vigour’. Then140 on the right portion of the boy’s hair the father puts three kuÅ›a bunches with the points towards the boy with the formula ‘herb, protect him’ (Tai. S. I. 2. 1. 1). With the words ‘Axe, do not harm him’ (Tai. S. I. 2. 1. 1) he presses a copper razor (on the kuÅ›a blades). The hair is cut141 with the mantra ‘with that razor with which Savitá¹›, the wise, cut (the hair) of king Soma and of Varuá¹‡a, cut now his (the boy’s hair), Oh brÄhmaá¹‡as, so that he may be endowed with long life and (reach) old age’. Each time the hair is cut, he gives the cut hair with their ends turned towards the east together with Å›amÄ« leaves to the mother, who puts them down on the bull dung. Cutting is done a second time with the mantra ‘with what DhÄtá¹› shaved (the head) of Bá¹›haspati, Agni and Indra for the [[263]] sake of their long life, with that I shave thy (head) for the sake of long life, fame and happiness’. The cutting is done a third time with the mantra ‘with what he may after night (is past) see the sun again and again, with that I shave thy (head) for the sake of long life, fame and happiness’. The cutting is done for the fourth time with all the three mantras together. Then the hair is cut three times on the left side similarly. The edge of the razor is then wiped off with the mantra ‘when thou shavest as a shaver the hair (of the boy) with the razor that wounds and is well-shaped purify his head, but do not deprive him of life’. Then he gives orders to the barber ‘doing with lukewarm water what has to be done with water, arrange his hair (well) without causing him (the boy) any wound’. Let him have the hair of the boy arranged according to the custom of the family. The rite only (without the mantras) is performed for a girl.142

According to several sÅ«tras, the cut hair placed in the dung of a bull is buried in a cow stable143 or is thrown in a pond or in the vicinity of water (PÄr., BhÄr.) or is buried at the root of the Udumbara tree (BhÄr.) or in a bunch of darbha grass (Baud., BhÄr., Gobhila) or in the forest (Gobhila). The MÄnava gá¹›. prescribes that as the hair falls down when cut they are gathered by some friendly person. The KÄá¹­haka gá¹›. and MÄnava gá¹›. say that the barber gets a sesame cake and a fine piece of cloth, while Vaik. says food is given to him. A bath for the boy is expressly prescribed by Baudh. and some others.

There is a great divergence of views about the number of locks of hair to be left on the head and the portion of the head where they are to be left. Baudh. gá¹›. says that one or three or five locks144 may be left on the head or according to family usage and he further says that some sages say that the locks should [[264]] be as many as the pravaras invoked by the father.145 Ä€Å›v. gá¹›., and PÄr. gá¹›. say that locks may be kept according to family usage. Ä€p. gá¹›. says that the locks may in number follow the pravara or they may be kept according to family usage. Gobhila and KhÄdira say that the locks should be arranged according to gotra and family usage. Whether they mean by ‘gotra’ the number of pravaras of the gotra or some rule such as the KÄá¹­haka gives is not clear. The KÄá¹­haka gá¹›. says that the VÄsiá¹£á¹­has keep a lock on the right, that persons of Atri and KaÅ›yapa gotra (or pravara) keep locks both on the right and the left, that the Bhá¹›gus shave the entire head, that the Ä€á¹…girasa gotra keeps five locks or only a line of hair, while persons of other gotras (like Agastya, ViÅ›vÄmitra &c.) keep a Å›ikhÄ (without any particular number of locks) simply because it is an auspicious sign or one may follow the usage of one’s family.146 Vaik.147 says that the locks may be one, two, three, five or seven according to the pravaras. á¹šgveda148 VII. 33. 1 refers to the fact that Vasiá¹£á¹­has had a lock of hair on the right side of the head and so the rule of the KÄá¹­haka has a very hoary antiquity behind it. Up to modern times one of the characteristic outward signs of all Hindus was the Å›ikhÄ (the top-knot). A verse of Devala says that whatever religious act a man does without the yajÃ±opavÄ«ta or without Å›ikhÄ is as good as undone and HÄrita rules that a person who cuts off his Å›ikhÄ through hate or ignorance or foolishness becomes pure only after [[265]] performing the taptaká¹›cchra penance.149 In the MudrÄrÄká¹£asa (I. 8) there is a reference to the Å›ikhÄ of CÄá¹‡akya having been kept untied when he was angered by the Nandas. Åšabara (on Jaimini I. 3. 7) remarks that the Å›ikhÄ (its position and locks) is a sign to indicate the gotra and quotes á¹šg. VI. 75. 17 (yatra bÄá¹‡Äá¸¥ &c. cited above in note 598). Vasiá¹£á¹­ha (II. 21) prescribes that members of all vará¹‡as (including the Å›Å«dra) should arrange their hair according to the fixed usage (of their family) or should shave the whole head except the Å›ikhÄ. A Vedic passage150 is ’the head that has no Å›ikhÄ on it is unholy’. For rules about the Å›ikhÄ of students vide later on under upanayana. During recent times men, particularly those receiving English education in towns and cities, are forsaking the ancient practice of keeping a Å›ikhÄ and follow the western method of allowing the hair to grow on the whole head.

In modern times the rite of cÅ«á¸Äkaraá¹‡a generally takes place if at all on the day of Upanayana.

Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. (I. 17. 18) expressly says that the ceremony of cÅ«á¸Äkaraá¹‡a was to be performed for girls also, but no Vedic mantras were to be repeated. Manu (II. 66) says that all the ceremonies from jÄtakarma to caula must be performed at the proper times for girls also in order to purify their bodies but without mantras and YÄj. (I. 13) is to the same effect. Even such late writers as MitramiÅ›ra say that the caula of girls may be performed according to the usage of the family and that their hair may be entirely shaved or a Å›ikhÄ may be kept or there should be no shaving at all.151

In some castes even in modern times girls when mere children are shaved once, it being supposed that the first hair are impure.

VidyÄrambha

VidyÄrambhaâ€”The gá¹›hya sÅ«tras and dharmasÅ«tras are entirely silent as to what was done for the child’s education [[266]] between the third year when usually caula was performed and the 8th year (from conception) when the upanayana usually took place (in the case of brÄhmaá¹‡as). They state that rarely upanayana was performed even in the 5th year (as will be shown hereafter). Some faint light is thrown on this matter by the ArthaÅ›Ästra of Kauá¹­ilya,152 which says that the prince after the performance of caula is to engage in the study of the alphabet and of arithmetic, and after his upanayana he is to study the Vedas, ÄnvÄ«ká¹£ikÄ« (metaphysics), vÄrtÄ (agriculture and the science of wealth) and daá¹‡á¸anÄ«ti (the art of government) up till the 16th year when the godÄna ceremony is to be performed and after which year he may marry. KÄlidÄsa also (in Raghuvaá¹ƒÅ›a III. 28) says that prince Aja first mastered the alphabet and then entered into the ocean of (Sanskrit) literature. BÄá¹‡a has probably the ArthaÅ›Ästra in view when he makes prince CandrÄpÄ«á¸a enter the temple of learning (vidyÄmandira) at 6 and remain there till he became sixteen and he (like Milton in his letter to Hartlib) tells us how extensive the ideal curriculum of studies in arts and sciences for the prince was thought to be.153 In the UttararÄmacarita (Act II) it is said that KuÅ›a and Lava were taught vidyÄs other than the Veda after their caula and before upanayana.

It appears that at least from the early centuries of the Christian era, a ceremony called VidyÄrambha (commencement of learning the alphabet) was celebrated. AparÄrka (pp. 30-31) and the Sm. C. (I. p. 26) cite verses from the MÄrkaá¹‡á¸eya-purÄá¹‡a154 [[267]] about vidyÄrambha as follows: in the fifth year of the child on some day from the 12th of the bright half of KÄrtika to the 11th of the bright half of Ä€á¹£Äá¸ha, but excluding the 1st, 6th, 8th, 15th tithi or rikta tithis (i. e. 4th, 9th and 14th) and Saturday and Tuesday, the ceremony of beginning to learn should be performed. Having worshipped Hari (Viá¹£á¹‡u), Laká¹£mÄ«, SarasvatÄ«, the sÅ«tra writers of one’s Å›ÄkhÄ and the lore peculiar to one’s family, one should offer in the fire oblations of clarified butter to the above mentioned deities and should honour brÄhmaá¹‡as by the payment of daká¹£iá¹‡Ä. The teacher should sit facing the east and the boy should face the west and the teacher should begin to teach the first lesson to the boy who should receive the benedictions of brÄhmaá¹‡as. Thereafter teaching should be stopped on the days of anadhyÄya (which will be specified later on).

The Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a (pp. 321-325) and Saá¹ƒskÄraratnamÄlÄ (pp. 904-907) have an extensive note, a considerable part of which is devoted to astrological matters. The Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a quotes passages from ViÅ›vÄmitra, Devala and other sages and works that vidyÄrambha is performed in the 5th year or in any case before upanayana. It also quotes a verse from Ná¹›siá¹ƒha that SarasvatÄ« and Gaá¹‡apati should be worshipped and then the teacher should be honoured. The modern practice is to begin learning the alphabet on an auspicious day, generally the 10th of the bright half of Ä€Å›vina. SarasvatÄ« and Gaá¹‡apati are worshipped, the teacher is honoured and the boy is asked to repeat the words ‘oá¹ƒ namaá¸¥ siddham’ and to write them on a slate and then he is taught the letters (‘a’, ‘Ä’ &c.) of the alphabet. The Saá¹ƒskÄraratnamÄlÄ155 calls this ceremony Aká¹£arasvÄ«kÄra (appropriately enough) and among other texts cites a long prose passage from Garga quoted in the PÄrijÄta and prescribes a homa also with ÄjyÄhutis to SarasvatÄ«, Hari, Laká¹£mÄ«, VighneÅ›a (Gaá¹‡apati), sÅ«trakÄras and one’s vidyÄ.

For detailed treatment of some of the {{sarskÃ¶ras|saá¹ƒskÄras}}, vide Dr. (Mrs.) Kamalabai Deshpande’s work ‘The Child in Ancient India’ (with copious references to the gá¹›hyasÅ«tras); Mrs. Stevenson’s ‘The Rites of the Twice-born’ (1920), which exhaustively reviews in the minutest details the rites of brÄhmaá¹‡as (particularly in Kathiawar and Gujarat) as observed at present. This work however gives hardly any references to original Sanskrit authorities, is permeated by the spirit of a Christian missionary and commits the mistake, usual with most Western writers, of comparing hoary Indian customs, usages and the position of women with those of the West only in the latter half of the 19th century, altogether ignoring what existed in Europe over a few hundred years ago, though it is generally written with sympathy and understanding. Colebrooke’s ‘Miscellaneous Essays’, Vol. I. pp. 123-226 (London, 1837), Monier Williams’ ‘Religious thought and life in India’ part I (1883), VidyÄrá¹‡ava’s ‘daily practices’ in the 20th volume of the ‘Sacred Books of the Hindus’ may also be consulted. ↩︎

  31. Besides the gá¹›hyasÅ«tras, the dharmasÅ«tras, Manu, YÄjÃ±avalkya and other smá¹›tis, the principal digests on saá¹ƒskÄra relied upon here are the {{Sangkaratattva|Saá¹ƒskÄratattva}} of Raghunandana, the {{SamskaramayÅ«kba|Saá¹ƒskÄramayÅ«kha}} of NÄ«lakaá¹‡á¹­ha, the {{Samskara-prakasa|Saá¹ƒskÄraprakÄÅ›a}} of MitramiÅ›ra, the {{Saá¹skÄraka ustubha|Saá¹ƒskÄrakaustubha}} of Anantadeva and the {{Samskararataamola|Saá¹ƒskÄraratnamÄlÄ}} of GopÄ«nÄtha. Further, one should never lose sight of the fact that in a vast continent like India the various items in daily rites and ceremonies have always varied from age to age, from province to province and from caste to caste. Innumerable modifications were introduced and usages cropped up among the people, particularly owing to the influence of women, of which smá¹›tis and digests take no notice. This was the state of things even several centuries before Christ. The Ä€p. Dh. S. (II. 11. 29. 15) closes with the aphorism ‘some teachers hold that the rest of the dharmas (not described here) may be understood from (the usages of) women and of all vará¹‡as’. The Ä€Å›v. gá¹›. (I. 7. 1) states ‘various indeed are the usages of the different countries and of the different villages; one should observe them in marriage ceremonies.’ This work does not profess to give the bewildering differences of the several Å›ÄkhÄs and the several provinces of Modern India, but will restrict itself principally to Western India and the Ä€Å›v. sÅ«tra, though important variations have been pointed out in many places. ↩︎

Vaik. says that the jar is to be to the south of the woman’s head; Ä€p. employs the word ‘Å›Ä«rá¹£ataá¸¥’ which SudarÅ›ana explains as ’near the head of the child’. The verse for the pot of water is ‘à¤†à¤ªà¥‹ à¤œà¤¾à¤—à¥ƒà¤¤ à¤¯à¤¥à¤¾ à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¥‡à¤·à¥ à¤œà¤¾à¤—à¥à¤°à¤¥ à¥¤ à¤à¤µà¤®à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ à¤¸à¥‚à¤¤à¤¿à¤•à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ à¤¸à¤ªà¥à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤¿à¤•à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤‚ à¤œà¤¾à¤—à¥à¤°à¤¥ à¥¥’ Ä€p. M. P. II. 13. 6 and Vaikh. III. 15 have a similar verse.}} ↩︎
